<tr id="pagespace" title="Ask HN: What is your Git commit&#x2F;push flow?" style="height:10px"></tr><tr><td><table class="fatitem" border="0">
        <tr class='athing' id='30712175'>
      <td align="right" valign="top" class="title"><span class="rank"></span></td>      <td valign="top" class="votelinks"><center><a id='up_30712175' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712175&amp;how=up&amp;auth=2986fc9497f8345fef814254d5f136f0f241326e&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center></td><td class="title"><a href="item?id=30712175" class="titlelink">Ask HN: What is your Git commit/push flow?</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2"></td><td class="subtext">
        <span class="score" id="score_30712175">180 points</span> by <a href="user?id=nineplay" class="hnuser">nineplay</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T15:27:51"><a href="item?id=30712175">13 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30712175"></span> | <a href="hide?id=30712175&amp;auth=2986fc9497f8345fef814254d5f136f0f241326e&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175">hide</a> | <a href="https://hn.algolia.com/?query=Ask%20HN%3A%20What%20is%20your%20Git%20commit%2Fpush%20flow%3F&type=story&dateRange=all&sort=byDate&storyText=false&prefix&page=0" class="hnpast">past</a> | <a href="fave?id=30712175&amp;auth=2986fc9497f8345fef814254d5f136f0f241326e">favorite</a> | <a href="item?id=30712175">163&nbsp;comments</a>              </td></tr>
      <tr style="height:2px"></tr><tr><td colspan="2"></td><td>I&#x27;ve long been in the practice of &quot;commit early, commit often&quot;.  If one use case works I commit, if the unit tests pass I commit. The code may be a mess, the variables may have names like &#x27;foo&#x27; and &#x27;bar&#x27; but I commit to have a last known good state.  If I start mass refactoring and break the unit tests, I can revert everything and start over.<p>I also push often because I&#x27;m forever aware disks can fail.  I&#x27;m not leaving a day&#x27;s worth of work on my local drive and hoping it&#x27;s there the next morning.<p>I&#x27;ve become increasingly aware that my coworkers have nice clean commit histories.  When I look at their PRs, there are 2-4 commits and each is a clean, completely functioning feature.  No &quot;fix misspellings and whitespace&quot; comments.<p>What flow do you follow?</td></tr>
        <tr style="height:10px"></tr><tr><td colspan="2"></td><td>

          <tr class='athing comtr' id='30712327'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30712327' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712327&amp;how=up&amp;auth=2c1421a3eedc9f212eb74d5ce3b669b6f7920e54&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712327'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=jawns" class="hnuser">jawns</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T15:39:14"><a href="item?id=30712327">13 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30712327"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712517" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30712327" n="38" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">Here&#x27;s how I address this problem.<p>When I&#x27;m developing, but before I create a PR, I&#x27;ll create a bunch of stream-of-consciousness commits.  This is stuff like &quot;Fix typo&quot; or &quot;Minor formatting changes&quot; mixed in with actual functional changes.<p>Right before I create the PR, or push up a shared branch, I do an interactive rebase (git rebase -i).<p>This allows me to organize my commits.  I can squash commits, amend commits, move commits around, rewrite the commit messages, etc.<p>Eventually I end up with the 2-4 clean commits that your coworkers have.  Often I design my commits around &quot;cherry-pick&quot; suitability.  The commit might not be able to stand on its own in a PR, but does it represent some reasonably contained portion of the work that could be cherry-picked onto another branch if needed?<p>Granted, all of the advice above requires you to adhere to a &quot;prefer rebase over merge&quot; workflow, and that has some potential pitfalls, e.g. you need to be aware of the Golden Rule of Rebasing:<p><a href="https:&#x2F;&#x2F;www.atlassian.com&#x2F;git&#x2F;tutorials&#x2F;merging-vs-rebasing#the-golden-rule-of-rebasing" rel="nofollow">https:&#x2F;&#x2F;www.atlassian.com&#x2F;git&#x2F;tutorials&#x2F;merging-vs-rebasing#...</a><p>But I vastly prefer this workflow to both &quot;merge only,&quot; where you can never get rid of those stream-of-consciousness commits, and &quot;squash everything,&quot; where every PR ends up with a single commit, even if it would be more useful to have multiple commits that could be potentially cherry-picked.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">

          <a href="user?id=elpakal" class="hnuser">elpakal</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T16:48:31"><a href="item?id=30713208">12 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713208"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712327" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">parent</a> | <a href="#30714226" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713208" n="2" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I do this (kind of too), but instead of an interactive rebase I just do a `git reset --soft &lt;target_branch&gt;`, where target_branch is the local (and up-to-date) copy of my target branch. That gets me one clean commit that I can force push up to replace my branch @ remote.<p>(This works for me because auditing commit history is not important where I work, if it were I would organize commits better.)</span>

                <tr class='athing comtr' id='30713793'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='5'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="200"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30713793' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713793&amp;how=up&amp;auth=7641a2c79bd145bc221002473aeec20860a1a418&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713793'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=OJFord" class="hnuser">OJFord</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:38:00"><a href="item?id=30713793">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713793"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712327" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">root</a> | <a href="#30713648" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">parent</a> | <a href="#30714559" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713793" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I suppose just the usual &#x27;rebase vs. merge&#x27; - i.e. if there are conflicts to resolve they will be dealt with commit-by-commit in the usual way when rebasing, whereas without the option set they will be dealt with all at once &#x27;merge-style&#x27;. I happen to think that&#x27;s a feature, but I know some don&#x27;t like it.<p>I think there&#x27;s less argument in favour of merge than usual with pulls though - since it&#x27;s much less like a semantic merge to begin with for git-merging to preserve a history of.<p>I&#x27;m certainly not aware of any <i>objective</i> downside&#x2F;gotcha&#x2F;&#x27;oh but it doesn&#x27;t work when...&#x27;, no.<p>The docs only add that you might further want to set it to `interactive` or `merges` rather than `true`, for the effect of rebasing with those options: <a href="https:&#x2F;&#x2F;git-scm.com&#x2F;docs&#x2F;git-config#Documentation&#x2F;git-config.txt-pullrebase" rel="nofollow">https:&#x2F;&#x2F;git-scm.com&#x2F;docs&#x2F;git-config#Documentation&#x2F;git-config...</a></span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">

      <center><a id='up_30714559' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714559&amp;how=up&amp;auth=b7d2e761e8abd61e4554e0fe040cb7176d6f580b&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714559'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">&gt; Why isn&#x27;t it on by default?<p>Because git was written primarily for the Linux kernel and the defaults reflect that. The workflow of the Linux kernel is completely different from what most people outside of it do with git. &quot;git pull&quot; is used for opposite purposes in both worlds.</span>

                  <span class="commtext c00">I do the same thing generally. In fact I generally find multiple mistakes or changes to be made, reading over my <i>own</i> code before merging, requiring figuring out which commit to merge my changes into (git absorb helps in the easy cases) and even more rebasing, or giving up and adding an extra commit at the end.<p>I see some people whose projects (Furnace Tracker, PipeWire, previously Famitudio) seem to make progress very quickly without getting noticeably slowed down by technical debt, despite sloppy programming and unorganized commit logs (push-to-head). Meanwhile I move slowly, dread reviewing hundreds of lines of my own code, and produce technical debt (regrets) anyway, not as many surface-level lintable errors but plenty of entrenched mistakes. I wish I could move faster, but instead struggle to make progress.</span>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30713057'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='2'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="80"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30713057' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713057&amp;how=up&amp;auth=0f9a4fecbf9fac35bfbcbf911c5c9185f529aa8c&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713057'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=btschaegg" class="hnuser">btschaegg</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T16:36:48"><a href="item?id=30713057">12 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713057"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712327" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">root</a> | <a href="#30712896" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">parent</a> | <a href="#30712957" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30717136" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713057" n="4" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">Have a look at `tig`. It&#x27;s even included in Git for Windows now and does this reasonably well.<p>Only the keybindings are a bit weird if you&#x27;re not accustomed to Vim bindings:<p>- Open tig<p>- Change into the staging view with `s`<p>- Select your file using the arrow or `j` and `k` keys<p>- Press Return to show the diff<p>- Navigate to the line(s) in question with `j` and `k` (arrow keys will switch files)<p>- Stage parts with `1` (single line), `2` (chunk parts), `u` (chunks) or split chunks with `\`<p>- &quot;Leave&quot; the diff with `q`<p>- You can find the keybindings with `h` in the help screen, which also uses Vim keys -- like manpages usually do</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30713057&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330713057">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>
        </table></td></tr>

      <center><a id='up_30713227' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713227&amp;how=up&amp;auth=4b16742c9c92c183f66e4c71708419848b22b282&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713227'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">&quot;s&quot; is status view, which you can also get at by running &quot;tig status&quot; directly.<p>&quot;c&quot; is staging view.<p>&gt; - Stage parts with `1` (single line), `2` (chunk parts), `u` (chunks) or split chunks with `\`<p>You can also revert a chunk or file by using &quot;!&quot;.       <center><a id='up_30717136' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30717136&amp;how=up&amp;auth=76883a8c61fe272e25b42be22dec226e4cce43b2&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30717136'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">I use Sourcetree [0] for this (on Mac but Win version available)<p>I&#x27;ve tried most Git clients on Mac over the years and kept gravitating back to Sourcetree.<p>I only tend to use it for this particular workflow (picking out very granular changes on a line-by-line basis).  Otherwise, 90% of my git stuff is via IDE integrations or command line.<p>[0] <a href="https:&#x2F;&#x2F;www.sourcetreeapp.com" rel="nofollow">https:&#x2F;&#x2F;www.sourcetreeapp.com</a></span>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30714428'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='2'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="80"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30714428' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714428&amp;how=up&amp;auth=058190e01681c28f9482b461831b3e4061a99f46&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714428'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=kjeetgill" class="hnuser">kjeetgill</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T18:35:20"><a href="item?id=30714428">10 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30714428"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712327" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">root</a> | <a href="#30712896" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">parent</a> | <a href="#30717136" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30714360" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30714428" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">If you&#x27;re a vim guy, I use `git difftool` setup with `vimdiff` for this. Let&#x27;s say you have your changes in a branch CHANGES on top of public branch PUBLIC.<p>1. I `git checkout PUBLIC -b CLEANUP` to a new branch.<p>2. Do a `git difftool CHANGES`, which opens each changed file in vimdiff one at a time.<p>3. For each file, I use :diffput&#x2F;:diffget or just edit in changes I want.<p>4. Commit these changes on the CLEANUP branch.<p>5. Use `git difftool CHANGES` again to see the remaining diff.<p>6. Repeat until the diff comes back empty!<p>My unstructured changes tend to contain a handful of small typo fixes, white spacing, localized refactors, and 1 or 2 larger refactors and a behavioral change.  Once they&#x27;re all broken out, It&#x27;s usually easy enough to use `git rebase -i` and reorder the smaller changes first, put out PRs for just those first, etc.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30714428&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330714428">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>
        </table></td></tr>

                  <tr class='athing comtr' id='30713392'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30713392' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713392&amp;how=up&amp;auth=a9d62a17b1af4f6cc45b375f0f49d513c1d3614f&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713392'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=lmarcos" class="hnuser">lmarcos</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:05:15"><a href="item?id=30713392">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713392"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30713776" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30713699" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713392" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I used to do exactly what you described a few years ago (when I was learning git for the first time). Not anymore. A few reasons:<p>- commit early&#x2F;commit often. I usually push one commit when I think the feature is done.    While others do a review of my code, I commit to improve the code&#x2F;fix the issues found by others. The advantage here is that future readers looking at the history of file X line N can know what other files were introduced alongside file X (as a reader of big codebases, this is a nice side effect). I don&#x27;t like hiding defects either from the git history (one could in theory squash all the commits of a given PR in order to keep the &quot;history clean&quot;... In my experience having a trace of bugs fixed at PR time, or other subtle details is also worth it and serves as documentation of what not to do).<p>In the cases I need to work through many days in a single feature, and only if the feature is so complicated&#x2F;critical than I cannot reproduce it from scratch by myself again, then yes I push the progress upstream. This is usually not the case though: I stash progress. I tend to open small PR and usually I remember what I&#x27;ve done (so I could write the entire code again easily). Plus, hard drives fail, sure but they are also quite reliable. In 20 years of work I never experienced losing &quot;non critical&quot; work because of disk failure (for critical work, I for sure have a different workflow).</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30713392&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330713392">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>
        </table></td></tr>
            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30713699'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30713699' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713699&amp;how=up&amp;auth=ff4b32553ffb694430ffb3fcb49e0a91965e730a&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713699'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=deckard1" class="hnuser">deckard1</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:30:16"><a href="item?id=30713699">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713699"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30713392" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30713032" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713699" n="3" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">The flow you use will typically depend on the company you are working for. Using regular git (no Github&#x2F;Gitlab) will often have a different workflow than Github.<p>My team (and myself) prefer this workflow:<p>- One commit per PR. This allows for easy reverts and cherry-pick.<p>- One developer per branch. You can do a few devs per branch, but rebases need to be coordinated and carefully handled, because:<p>- No merge commits. Only rebase onto latest main. Which means force-pushing PR branches and, thus, rewriting history (other devs working on same branch need to be aware that history has changed).<p>If you&#x27;re constantly rebasing onto main, then all of your working commits sit on top of the latest code in main. Which means you do not have to deal with tricky merge conflicts, where your commits may weave in and out of the main branch at various points in time because you were doing &quot;git merge&quot; at random points. In addition, if you squash your commits before doing a rebase this will <i>also</i> make merge conflicts rather trivial, because you&#x27;re only dealing with one set of merge conflicts on one commit.<p>That&#x27;s the big picture, team workflow. For my <i>personal</i> workflow, I rely on &quot;git add -p&quot; and stashes. The only time I do a commit and push up code is: a) when I have a large change and want to make sure I don&#x27;t lose it <i>or</i> b) others have already reviewed my PR and I want to keep the new changes separate to make their life easier when reviewing a 2nd time. I use &quot;git reset --soft HEAD~&lt;number-of-commits&gt;&quot; to squash instead of &quot;git rebase -i&quot; because I find it easier and quicker.<p>I must emphasize this point: learn &quot;git add -p&quot;. It&#x27;s <i>extremely</i> useful in the case where you have some changes like debugging code or random unrelated changes that you do not want to commit. It&#x27;s a filtering mechanism.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30713699&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330713699">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>
        </table></td></tr>

          <a href="user?id=wodenokoto" class="hnuser">wodenokoto</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:42:17"><a href="item?id=30713841">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713841"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30713699" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">parent</a> | <a href="#30713032" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713841" n="2" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">

      <center><a id='up_30718488' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30718488&amp;how=up&amp;auth=bdd0b75c83975d9eed2c1066f166324d4df29504&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30718488'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

Working in their same branch to add that for their environment&#x2F;branch was much faster than trying to coordinate a series of other changes&#x2F;branches.  It&#x27;s not something I&#x2F;we do a lot, but the few times we&#x27;ve done it in the last couple of months people have really liked it, and seemed to feel like we were making faster progress somehow.</span>

                        <tr class='athing comtr' id='30713032'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30713032' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713032&amp;how=up&amp;auth=4cd18f22d45c98cdb7ba22eb196aa963b4247e64&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713032'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=izietto" class="hnuser">izietto</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T16:34:35"><a href="item?id=30713032">12 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713032"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30713699" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30712998" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713032" n="2" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">My flow is:<p>1.a. a lot of dirty commits&#x2F;wip commits<p>1.b. a few of clean commits, when I spot changes that I know they are already a commit by itself<p>Before opening the PR:<p>2. `git log -p`: I inspect the commits I&#x27;ve done and I decide what should go together, what should be edited and what can stay as it is<p>3. `git rebase -i`: I apply the changes I&#x27;ve decided during 2<p>4. repeat 2 and 3 until I&#x27;m happy with the results<p>5. the last `git rebase -i`: reword almost every commit, as almost all the commits at this point have placeholder descriptions<p>I&#x27;m very happy with this strategy. It requires some time to get used to it but at the end my PRs are very clean and well-thought.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30713032&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330713032">reply</a></u>

                  <span class="commtext c00">One thing that can make rebasing much easier is making use of --fixup and --squash. Often you know at the time that a commit is either fixing a previous commit or that you would want to squash it with a previous commit. This can save a lot of time later as you can simply issue a --autosquash to rebase. If you do it right it means others can rebase your branch too.</span>

      <center><a id='up_30712998' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712998&amp;how=up&amp;auth=f6734fa27ce9d05a0968d23ef545f8aac6279a52&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712998'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">If you are afraid to lose what you&#x27;ve done, you can stash your minor changes to keep track of the small things that you already got working, and then, when you got enough working to make a commit, you do it with the best code you got. And for the many pushes, you could backup your projects files everyday. I think is a much more appropriate way to use the tools that git gives us.<p>That way, you won&#x27;t be afraid to lose your recent work by messing something up, because you have the stash, and won&#x27;t be afraid to lose your whole project&#x2F;progress because you have a recente backup of it.<p>For instance, I have a backup script that runs everytime I shutdown my work computer so I won&#x27;t have to worry if suddenly my hard drive gives up on everything.</span>

                              <tr class='athing comtr' id='30715217'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30715217' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30715217&amp;how=up&amp;auth=c3de73265fce6ced3455e48f75348484fb5ff396&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30715217'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=ghotli" class="hnuser">ghotli</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T19:54:14"><a href="item?id=30715217">9 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30715217"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30714246" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30712383" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30715217" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I barely skimmed what&#x27;s below so maybe someone else has covered this, maybe not.<p>If I need to hack on a task and I know I&#x27;m the only person that&#x27;s going to work on the branch I amend changes to my commit basically constantly. The only reason I might not is if I want multiple commits on the branch to make review simpler for others.<p>```<p>git fetch &amp;&amp; git checkout main<p>git branch feature&#x2F;JIRA-###&#x2F;words-describing-it<p>git checkout feature&#x2F;JIRA-###&#x2F;words-describing-it<p>&lt;do some work&gt;<p>git add .<p>git commit -m &quot;JIRA-###: words &#x2F; description&quot;<p>git push -u origin feature&#x2F;JIRA-###&#x2F;words-
describing-it<p>&lt;review CI build, make more changes&gt;<p>git add .<p>git amend<p>git push -f<p>```<p>`git amend` is an alias in my ~&#x2F;.gitconfig<p>```<p>#cat ~&#x2F;.gitconfig<p>&lt;snip&gt;<p>[alias]<p><pre><code>        amend = commit --amend --no-edit
</code></pre>
```<p>So most of my commits I just amend the first commit I made, force push, review CI build results, rinse repeat until it&#x27;s ready for review.<p>Simplest thing that works when it&#x27;s just my own work on a branch. I&#x27;ll also do a rebase pre PR review if need be.<p>```<p>git fetch &amp;&amp; git rebase -i origin&#x2F;main<p>git status<p>&lt;do what it says if anything&gt;<p>git add &lt;fixed files&gt;<p>git rebase --continue<p>git push -f<p>```</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30715217&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330715217">reply</a></u>
                  </font>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30712383'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30712383' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712383&amp;how=up&amp;auth=9a8124a40f9ddd7421bce48c8bec4b6ffcb18f22&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712383'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=jmchuster" class="hnuser">jmchuster</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T15:43:24"><a href="item?id=30712383">13 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30712383"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30715217" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30713388" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30712383" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">Create a feature branch for everything i work on. Then squash all commits on that branch for merge.  If that doesn&#x27;t look clean because there are too many features munged together, then split that feature branch into multiple ones (ideally would have already realized this ahead of time), then squash and merge each of those.  So, then the main branch commit history is only &quot;clean, completely functioning features&quot; and then a link back to the PR that then has the full history of commits that got squashed, if you wanted more insight into the development process.  The github UI simplifies a lot of this process and makes it a lot nicer to view.</span>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30713388'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30713388' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713388&amp;how=up&amp;auth=bcf3477f72dce8d5c52c563248ea2b4c6a77e196&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713388'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=jedimastert" class="hnuser">jedimastert</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:04:49"><a href="item?id=30713388">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713388"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712383" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30718414" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713388" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">The git history on my personal&#x2F;dev-machine are an absolute mess that I try to optimize for `git bisect`, so preferably each commit should compile (and I usually commit whenever a new part of the requirements is introduced&#x2F;a new test passes)<p>The way our code review system works is essentially each upload is a commit, so the review&#x27;s history is like a compressed version of the local git history. I usually upload when I think the branch is &quot;complete&quot;&#x2F;will pass all of the CI, and ~once per round of comments.<p>The main git history is squash-and-rebase, so one commit per code-review, with the CR description serving as the commit message (with automatic links to bugs and code reviews and the like).<p>I personally like the squash-and-merge strategy for the main branch, it makes the most sense (especially when the code review&#x27;s history is still around after)</span>

      <center><a id='up_30712288' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712288&amp;how=up&amp;auth=e6766a815d87a3328f5dc43607c4074295a70955&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712288'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">Yeah.<p>Consider I have a feature branch that no-one depends on. When my history is messed up I do easy rebase :<p><pre><code>  git reset --soft master (or whatever commit up to which I want to UNDO, but no further than commit I branched off from)
</code></pre>
Now at this point changes from multiple commits are in my STAGING.<p><pre><code>  git commit -m &quot;Feature X implemented&quot;
  git push --force (you can do If no-one depends on your branch, otherwise - don&#x27;t do this)</code></pre></span>

                  <tr class='athing comtr' id='30715885'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30715885' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30715885&amp;how=up&amp;auth=7f523b7c1a965d94fae39a062a2c225bcbbecfae&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30715885'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=michaelsalim" class="hnuser">michaelsalim</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T21:06:16"><a href="item?id=30715885">7 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30715885"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712245" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30712421" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30715885" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I commit as soon as a single functionality makes sense as its own. Simple example:
If function X uses function A, B and C. I&#x27;ll commit after finishing A, B, or C. And once X is then, I create a separate commit. I also ensure the code still runs most of the time. How I group them depends on how complicated it is. The more complex, the more commits.<p>This helps me write code that are more standalone (easier to replace &amp; test). Also helps to clear the cognitive burden when developing larger features. Anything that&#x27;s committed are pretty much &quot;done&quot;. So if I leave any work mid-way, I can simply look at the untracked files to know where I was at.<p>Then at the end of the work&#x2F;PR, I go through every changes to make sure everything makes sense.<p>In practice, this means that the first code I write is often the last I commit (see my simple example above).</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30715885&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330715885">reply</a></u>
                  </font>

      <center><a id='up_30712421' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712421&amp;how=up&amp;auth=1d04448d0503ca30942b577f60f565f66efc3ad2&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712421'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">I often commit as well to checkpoint, and push those onto a branch organized under my username as a backup (&lt;username&gt;&#x2F;&lt;branch&gt; to make use of ref folders). When I’m done, I use interactive rebase to clean the branch up, writing meaningful commit messages for each unit of change (answering the question “why does this commit exist?” usually). I force push that cleaned up branch and then open a PR.<p>My view is that final commits are a form of communication, and deserve some intention. I’ve thanked myself when I’ve looked years back at work I’ve done and been able to figure out not only the change, but also my own state of mind.</span>

                  <span class="commtext c00">I see no value in shorter &quot;clean&quot; commit histories in a feature branch. It doesn&#x27;t matter, its going to get rebased&#x2F;squashed to a single commit in the merge process anyway (which IMO everyone should be doing, true merges from feature branches to mainline are bad).</span>

      <center><a id='up_30713804' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713804&amp;how=up&amp;auth=fa857ec542bcdd2b4057a1e1fb80d13c0746e549&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713804'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=meowface" class="hnuser">meowface</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:39:11"><a href="item?id=30713804">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713804"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30713538" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">root</a> | <a href="#30713734" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">parent</a> | <a href="#30713760" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713804" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">Yep. Dropbox is also a great safety net for when I mess something up in between commits. I&#x27;ve had a few times where I&#x27;ve deleted or changed some code that I couldn&#x27;t restore just via undos. Normally I&#x27;d pretty much be screwed, but Dropbox retains a diff every time a file is saved, so it&#x27;s basically impossible for me to lose anything.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">

      <center><a id='up_30714480' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714480&amp;how=up&amp;auth=0cd8c9e39d83abe8a343c82c40f62ea7ec4155f4&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714480'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">I have three branches: macbook, imac, and develop.<p>develop is the mainline branch that other people could pull from.<p>macbook and imac are each backups of their respective machines.  I use git merge --ff-only to rebase commits from one to the other when I change machines.  If I need to change machines midway through a commit, I&#x27;ll push a WIP commit to imac, fastforward macbook to match, and then git reset HEAD^ to keep the WIP files (but undo the commit) on macbook.  I finish on macbook, make the commit, and push it to origin&#x27;s macbook and develop.<p>Like the others here, I make liberal use of git rebase -i to reorder commits, etc.  If imac and macbook ever diverge (e.g. I forgot to push a commit before changing machines), I also use interactive rebase to resolve it.</span>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30712664'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30712664' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712664&amp;how=up&amp;auth=53e738bf5d1da03d41d3a92fccb80c3f197632e2&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712664'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=crnkofe" class="hnuser">crnkofe</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T16:05:23"><a href="item?id=30712664">12 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30712664"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30714480" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30717817" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30712664" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">The n1 rule I follow (and push everyone to follow) is: &quot;Don&#x27;t write shitty commit messages.&quot; It helps in general with everything related to Git.<p>Otherwise I decide ahead of time and focus on one area at a time that I know is a small chunk of functionality (like adding boilerplate for a small script, unit testing a piece of functionality, implementing an endpoint) and as long as I&#x27;m working on it I just use git add -A ; git commit --amend --no-edit . Once I move on to new self-contained batch of functionality I make a new commit and repeat the amending. It requires a bit of discipline to keep commits small enough but I like it since it&#x27;s an easy process to follow.<p>Before I make a review request I usually do an interactive rebase and reword commit messages, sometimes reorder them and squash small stuff if it makes sense. After a review is in process I generally no longer rebase (to keep review transparent) and commit each fix separately.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">

                  <span class="commtext c00">Not entirely unlike yours, but I also very often use `git commit --fixup` and let the computer clean up large portions of my messes with `git rebase -i --autosquash` later.<p>That is, nowadays I usually do the same, but through Git Fork [1] (via its custom command system), and it works even better than the terminal interface for me -- which doesn&#x27;t happen often. But for fixups the &quot;find the target commit in the history and use a context menu entry to flag your commit flow&quot; really works rather nicely.<p>Also, Fork&#x27;s interactive rebasing dialog does `--autosquash` by default.<p>[1]: <a href="https:&#x2F;&#x2F;git-fork.com" rel="nofollow">https:&#x2F;&#x2F;git-fork.com</a></span>

      <center><a id='up_30714797' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714797&amp;how=up&amp;auth=d5a92b9c897b559669fbaae194cb4e5f7bb1b656&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714797'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">I try to make clean commits that do one thing only. If I have trouble writing a meaningful commit message, it means I have failed and should do a better job next time.<p>That said, I often make a messy &quot;wip&quot; commit that I push to my branch, just so that the work doesn&#x27;t get lost. But I always undo such a commit and clean it up.<p>Also, I always use git add -p, so that I can break changes into multiple meaningful commits and review them one more time before pushing.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30714023'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30714023' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714023&amp;how=up&amp;auth=e3bb39729ce96645c0079f576758f725ee5a0a95&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714023'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=rendall" class="hnuser">rendall</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:56:11"><a href="item?id=30714023">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30714023"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30714797" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30712355" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30714023" n="2" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I say, you have it right and your coworkers are less so. Encourage your coworkers to be secure in their work. Show your struggles, false starts, mistakes, typo fixes. Embrace the messy history!<p>My last few teams&#x27; workflows and projects were such that commit history wasn&#x27;t really a big deal. I&#x27;m skeptical that it matters if the dev-to-production process is smooth.<p>These tips are habits we have:<p>Keep commit message first line to under 50 characters, use imperative mood, capitalized. If you need more than that to describe what&#x27;s going on then the commit might be too big.<p>PRs must pass all continuous integration tests + 1 or 2 devs approval. Pretty much always do what a dev suggests in review, even if you disagree.<p>Optionally, squash merge PRs to master, to make reverting easier. I prefer that.<p>If a feature is very complicated, break it down into smaller tasks, merging each to master when completed.<p>Maybe use a feature branch for super complicated inextricably tasks, but doing that too often is a code smell.<p>In that situation, code history isn&#x27;t really that important.<p>That said, if you&#x27;re still keen, look up these commands:<p><pre><code>  git commit --fixup {commit id}
  git reset --soft {something}
  git rebase -i --autosquash {commit id}
</code></pre>
Lots of force pushing, will be needed, but never, ever force push to a shared branch unless every one agrees and is aware.<p>Those can straighten out your history right out.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30714023&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330714023">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>

                  <tr class='athing comtr' id='30712355'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30712355' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712355&amp;how=up&amp;auth=d92f87672fc3f85f99770b5badcbe80102413e69&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712355'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=keehun" class="hnuser">keehun</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T15:41:02"><a href="item?id=30712355">13 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30712355"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30714023" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30716768" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30712355" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I do exactly as you say: commit early and commit often. However, something I find quite successful when publishing a Pull Request (aka CR&#x2F;MR), especially large ones, is that I&#x27;ll use git rebase to group chunk diffs together in a logical flow to create an easy-to-review set of commits.<p>If I have 5 commits that are all whitespace changes, I&#x27;ll use git rebase to group them into one commit before I push.<p>If it&#x27;s a large, complex PR, then I&#x27;ll reorder my commits and pick-and-choose chunks from the many commits so that they make sense in a progression. For example, I might just add one new component in the first commit. Add a second component in the second commit. Third commit might be integrating those two components into the system.<p>If my PR consists of both &quot;cleanup&quot; like running black on python, then I&#x27;ll make that its own commit instead of littering actual functional changes within cosmetic ones.<p>I try to make it as easy as possible for my reviewers to review my code and actually catch mistakes. I also tell the reviewers to look at the commits separately and not to look at the entire diff which can sometimes be hundreds of thousands of lines of code. Even a large PR with 500+ lines of changes can be easy to review if you structure the commits right. (It&#x27;s really like many PRs within one PR.)<p>You may ask, why not have separate PRs then for each commit? Sometimes, that makes sense when it&#x27;s so large that it&#x27;s worth having an integration branch that lasts weeks or months. Sometimes, it&#x27;s not possible to just merge that one commit due to problems it&#x27;ll cause with compilation or linting or other issues. Also, with separate PRs, it can be more costly (in both CICD resources and the dev&#x27;s time) to do automated testing on each commit separately.<p>With some practice, the git rebasing and reordering or restructuring commits is not as difficult as it sounds, especially with the aid of a good git GUI (I like Fork.app) that lets me choose exactly the chunk to commit from a particular diff.<p>Once I got really comfortable with this workflow, what I&#x27;ll actually starting doing is... when I&#x27;m done with something and ready to put up a PR, I&#x27;ll flatten (fixup) all my commits into one, and then I&#x27;ll revert it and commit that. Then I revert the revert but instead of committing that revert-the-revert, I&#x27;ll stage it. This gives me all of my changes in a staged state. Then I&#x27;ll pick-and-choose chunks from that entire diff to craft a &quot;narrative&quot; like I was describing before. Once I have everything in the right order, I&#x27;ll drop the first two commits to get of the initial and revert commits.</span>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30714048'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30714048' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714048&amp;how=up&amp;auth=ec12c558693bd6495bfc2c0cb29a02d069833c34&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714048'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=chaitanya" class="hnuser">chaitanya</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:58:27"><a href="item?id=30714048">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30714048"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30716768" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30712365" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30714048" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">You can have the best of both worlds -- &quot;commit early, commit often&quot;, and &quot;nice clean commit histories&quot; -- with git, and it is easier than most people think.<p>- So you start work on a new branch, and reach a checkpoint. Create a new commit with &quot;git commit&quot;.<p>- Continue working, and when you reach the next checkpoint, create another commit. But this time, use &quot;git commit --amend&quot;. Contrary to what the flag says, this doesn&#x27;t modify the previous commit, instead it modifies your commit history and replaces the last commit with a new one[1]. So instead of having two new commits in your branch&#x27;s history, you only have one.<p>- Repeat the process until you have something worth pushing to the remote.<p>- Once you&#x27;ve pushed to remote, remember to not make any further modifications to your git history[2] i.e. going forward, create a new commit and only then run &quot;git commit --amend&quot;.<p>Now, there&#x27;s an obvious question here: if &quot;git commit --amend&quot; keeps replacing the last commit with a new one, what happens when you mess things up and want to revert to NOT the last checkpoint, but some checkpoint before that?<p>The trick here that git has up its sleeve is called &quot;git reflog&quot;. You see, while &quot;git commit --amend&quot; replaces the last commit in your branch&#x27;s history with a new one, the older commit is not actually gone, its still there. And you can see all of them with &quot;git reflog&quot;. Basically &quot;git reflog&quot; returns every commit at which the local HEAD (i.e. the commit checked out in your working directory) has been. So none of the commits that you replaced with --amend are actually lost, and you can find them all in the reflog.<p>Restoring to an older checkpoint becomes as easy as running &quot;git reset --hard &lt;previous-commit-id&gt;&quot;, or if you want to play safe, &quot;git checkout -b &lt;new-branch-name&gt; &lt;previous-commit-id&gt;&quot;.<p>Hope this helps!<p>Notes:<p>1. By design, a commit, or in fact most objects in git cannot be modified. They are immutable.<p>2. Basically you can play with git history as long as it is private to you and not shared with others. But the moment you push it to a remote you share with others it&#x27;s no longer private and you must not modify that history anymore. Read Linus&#x27;s note on keeping a clean git history: <a href="https:&#x2F;&#x2F;www.mail-archive.com&#x2F;dri-devel@lists.sourceforge.net&#x2F;msg39091.html" rel="nofollow">https:&#x2F;&#x2F;www.mail-archive.com&#x2F;dri-devel@lists.sourceforge.net...</a></span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30714048&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330714048">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30715219'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30715219' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30715219&amp;how=up&amp;auth=86760ebebc189e1d4d787293b81dba41ea82a61c&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30715219'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=camnora" class="hnuser">camnora</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T19:54:27"><a href="item?id=30715219">9 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30715219"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30715701" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30713909" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30715219" n="2" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I tend to keep my PRs small enough to where several can be submitted within a day. This being said, I tend to keep my changes un-staged.<p>When I&#x27;m ready to commit:<p>1. `git diff` to get an overall picture of what changes were made. Which parts of this diff can be packaged into an isolated commit?<p>2. `git add -p` This is where I selectively stage bits.<p>3. `git diff --cached` to verify that the staged items are all in place<p>4. `git commit` with a detailed message.<p>5. Repeat steps 1-4 until all changes have been committed.<p>6. `git fetch origin main &amp;&amp; git rebase origin&#x2F;main`<p>7. Finally, `git push`<p>When PR feedback is left by peers, some teammates prefer you to not rewrite commits and force push. This makes re-review easier for them (especially if you use the Github features around PR review).<p>I opt for rewriting commits if it&#x27;s okay with team members. This way you don&#x27;t have &quot;fix typo&quot; commits getting merged into the main branch.<p>Edit: formatting</span>

                  <tr class='athing comtr' id='30713909'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30713909' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30713909&amp;how=up&amp;auth=5a9c1b27f804c7740627d444c691a14b567cce27&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30713909'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=smcameron" class="hnuser">smcameron</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:46:57"><a href="item?id=30713909">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30713909"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30715219" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30714894" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30713909" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I use stgit[1] which makes it easy to polish patches (commits), re-order patches, format and email patches, etc. It also makes it very easy to keep a lot of balls in the air at once, working on lots of commits all at the same time while keeping them reasonably separated from one another.  I&#x27;ve had maybe 50 to 100 patches going at once from time to time (but usually more like 5 to 10).  Then there are guys like Andrew Morton who&#x27;s known to juggle 1000s of patches at a time using ancient home grown scripts that are the primordial ancestor of quilt[2] and stgit.<p>[1] <a href="https:&#x2F;&#x2F;stacked-git.github.io&#x2F;" rel="nofollow">https:&#x2F;&#x2F;stacked-git.github.io&#x2F;</a>
[2] <a href="https:&#x2F;&#x2F;en.wikipedia.org&#x2F;wiki&#x2F;Quilt_(software)" rel="nofollow">https:&#x2F;&#x2F;en.wikipedia.org&#x2F;wiki&#x2F;Quilt_(software)</a><p>Edit: forgot to mention, once I&#x27;m happy with a given patch&#x2F;commit, or series of patches&#x2F;commits, I then cherry-pick or merge them from my patches branch over to the &quot;real&quot; branch, and then push them.</span>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30714894'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30714894' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714894&amp;how=up&amp;auth=6dd0df592daed4dd43ad13c4df34f20a1fccdff1&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714894'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=shoo" class="hnuser">shoo</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T19:21:15"><a href="item?id=30714894">9 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30714894"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30713909" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30714388" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30714894" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">always use `git add -p`. review each individual hunk before staging it into commit. dont blindly add everything.<p>once you&#x27;ve got it working, do a second pass on the commit history and compress it into one or more coherent logical commits, reordering or squashing changes as necessary. learn the interactive rebase interface. lots of operations of the form `git rebase -i HEAD^^^^^^`<p>where necessary, if you need to erase your old messy history from a feature branch and overwrite it with your tidied up commits, `git push --force-with-lease some-remote some-feature-branch`.   in general, avoid using `--force`, and if you ever believe you need `--force`, prefer `--force-with-lease` . the latter avoids some failure modes related to race conditions where a coworker pushes some commits and your concurrent `--force` push that lands a few seconds later blows them all away.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30714894&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330714894">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30714388'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30714388' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714388&amp;how=up&amp;auth=3865bc352521134cd0ecf935fe925e2cd193ae0c&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714388'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=WorldMaker" class="hnuser">WorldMaker</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T18:31:22"><a href="item?id=30714388">10 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30714388"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30714894" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30712869" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30714388" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">My opinion is that the time for clean commits is the merge commits created by PRs. Git uses a two dimensional commit data structure and most git commands today take a --first-parent argument that gives you a smart, clean view of just the merge commits. It&#x27;s only a bit of config work to make those your default aliases, and I just wish more UI tools defaulted to something more like it.<p>I don&#x27;t necessarily care how &quot;messy&quot; the commits inside a PR are at that point if my default view is at the merge commit&#x2F;PR level.<p>Teaching your coworkers how to use --first-parent may be beneficial to everyone and less stress than worrying about the peer pressure to conform to smaller &quot;cleaner&quot; commits.<p>That said, making cleaner commits is sometimes a useful skillset to learn for <i>yourself</i>. Sure, it can make code reviews easier, but it&#x27;s advanced skills that take practice and most PR tools aren&#x27;t particularly good at reviewing a commit at a time anyway so most PR reviews are entire PR at a time. I use tools like git add -p and git rebase --interactive as I feel necessary to clean up a commit narrative, but I also have a strong understanding of when not to force push and have years of &quot;training&quot; in these commands. I don&#x27;t expect and don&#x27;t want to expect junior devs to use them. I&#x27;m happy with the --first-parent approach to the DAG. I&#x27;m also always happy to teach junior devs how to use git add -p and git rebase --interactive, but when it is for <i>them</i>, when it is they who want to improve their skills.<p>A semi-related thing here is that the other way to remove a lot of &quot;fix misspellings and whitespace&quot; commits is to automate them away. Standardized formatters like prettier remove most of the manual effort of whitespace management. They can be setup to format on save in editors like VS Code. They can be added to pre-commit hooks. Similarly, many misspelling problems (certainly not all) get automated away with type systems and linters.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30714388&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330714388">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>
        </table></td></tr>

                  <span class="commtext c00">I commit often (as long as it compiles, pass the linter, and fast unit tests if there exist), then I rewrite the history. If I work alone on the feature (which is most case) I also push quite often on a private branch (mostly for backup, and for running the CI). If needed, I also rewrite the history of my private branch and `push -f` (even though this isn&#x27;t ideal...).<p>My PRs are clean, each commit pass the CI and is its own thing.</span>

                  <span class="commtext c00">I got conditioned to commit early and commit often for much of the same reasons, hardware failures while dealing with code that touched hardware. GPU’s mainly. So I would commit and push to have CI do checks while I pushed more code changes so that I could reduce the cycle.<p>A few years ago, Acme Security Corp decided to use my commit frequency as an excuse to say “I didn’t know how to code…” and let me go. So really it’s all over the spectrum and the correct answer is, whatever your team is doing.<p>If they are holding back commits for uber PR’s, well, so should you. Are they in a commit frenzy and everyone PR’s all day long? So should you.</span>

                  <span class="commtext c00">My main git case is idiosyncratic -- I use blogdown [0] to host my personal blog, which means I am first pushing the content to GitHub and then it gets built&#x2F;deployed on Netlify.<p>I typically commit once at first as a pretty complete draft, and then every subsequent edit&#x2F;revision is a commit and rebuild. This comes to 20-30 commits per post, and some are indeed like &#x27;fixed a spelling error&#x27;.<p>For this case, the benefits of being a frequent commiter clearly outweigh the costs.<p>[0] <a href="https:&#x2F;&#x2F;bookdown.org&#x2F;yihui&#x2F;blogdown&#x2F;" rel="nofollow">https:&#x2F;&#x2F;bookdown.org&#x2F;yihui&#x2F;blogdown&#x2F;</a></span>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30712723'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30712723' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712723&amp;how=up&amp;auth=d323785391082fe9e2f026baf086f9f9b0d084f5&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712723'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=_se" class="hnuser">_se</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T16:10:47"><a href="item?id=30712723">12 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30712723"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30715140" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30712587" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30712723" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">I think part of it depends on the tooling that you&#x27;re using and what your review system supports. Assuming that these are all supported, I would generally:<p>1. Push very often, essentially every time I&#x27;m going to context switch or take a break<p>2. Iterate often, get things working, then clean them up, pushing at each step along the way<p>3. Interactive rebase to squash into meaningful stacked commits for review<p>4. Mark the change as ready for review by my team<p>I have found stacked commits to be the best way to both perform and author reviews by a wide margin. It&#x27;s so much easier to review 4 200 line semantic changes than a single 800 line change. You&#x27;ll get better feedback from others, and thinking about development in this way can also lead to better results on its own.</span>

                      <u><a href="reply?id=30712723&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330712723">reply</a></u>

      <center><a id='up_30712587' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712587&amp;how=up&amp;auth=d3298a09a245cda4df4bee5b9109ea610b7da7df&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712587'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">I do the same as you - commit early and often, push nearly on every or every few commits, but I try and write my commit messages nicely even (and especially) if the code behind them is not so clean.<p>I care less about the code quality during development, so long as the commits make sense and the end PR is clean. Commits that may have placeholder names, disorganized code could include:<p>&quot;fix: failing test case&quot;
&quot;feat: support new user flow&quot;
&quot;cleanup: variable names and helper functions&quot;
&quot;cleanup: organize files&quot; 
etc<p>If the commit messages are clear as to why the change was committed, reviewing is a lot easier even with less than ideal code along the way. So long as the end state is cleaned up.</span>

      <center><a id='up_30712514' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712514&amp;how=up&amp;auth=65a2b362383e5b7bea7d957d72fbddf0ab46b779&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712514'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=mkirsche" class="hnuser">mkirsche</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T15:53:23"><a href="item?id=30712514">13 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30712514"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712287" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">root</a> | <a href="#30712422" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">parent</a> | <a href="#30716203" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30714041" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30712514" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">In addition it makes rebasing harder, which might make you use merge commits and then reading your commits gets even worse because changes might hide inside those merge commits.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30712514&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330712514">reply</a></u>

                        <tr class='athing comtr' id='30714041'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30714041' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30714041&amp;how=up&amp;auth=e80acba08de3b0fbcc2028597ce5f36f29c0940a&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30714041'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=dspillett" class="hnuser">dspillett</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T17:57:46"><a href="item?id=30714041">11 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30714041"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712287" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30713665" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30714041" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00"><i>&gt; The code may be a mess, the variables may have names like &#x27;foo&#x27; and &#x27;bar&#x27; but I commit to have a last known good state. If I start mass refactoring and break the unit tests, I can revert everything and start over.</i><p>For that use case, if you are sure you don&#x27;t want the intervening history in the main source repository, is to keep your “scratch” commits to a local store. This could be a branch, or a separate repo.<p>For my work areas I have a timed rsync taking snapshots, and can kick it off manually if I want to make a snapshot for a specific time (just finished debugging a major function, for instance). If there have been no changes since it last run the snapshot is not kept (just a note added to a file to say one was considered at that time). Identical files are all hard-links to the same data so it is pretty space efficient unless you have some large assets that change regularly. This has the advantage that I don&#x27;t even have to remember to commit to anything: the snapshot is <i>automatically</i> taken regularly. Rolling back is manual, but rarely needs to be done and can be done easily for small parts of the update if the rest needs to stay.<p>Every now and then, as this is just backing up temporary work status, clear down snapshots older than a given point in time. Using a source control repo and auto-committing to that would work as well as creating filesystem based snapshots like I do I should think, but all my other backups were rsync+snapshot based when I set this up so I just repurposed existing scripts for the job.<p><i>&gt; I&#x27;m forever aware disks can fail. I&#x27;m not leaving a day&#x27;s worth of work on my local drive and hoping it&#x27;s there the next morning.</i><p>Keep the snapshots on a different drive, or even a different machine (they are mostly in my case, though “just because it happened to get laid out that way” rather than by thoughtful design). Mine are even covered by daily off-site backups so if the place burns down overnight I still don&#x27;t lose them (or at least absolutely no more than a day&#x27;s worth). Just be careful with rsync, if you use this method, due to the many hard-links as the --hard-links option can be quite CPU and memory intensive, and if your backups are usually append-only you&#x27;ll need to occasionally wipe old snapshots that are long since irrelevant.</span>
              <div class='reply'>        <p><font size="1">
                      <u><a href="reply?id=30714041&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175%2330714041">reply</a></u>
                  </font>
      </div></div></td></tr>

                  <span class="commtext c00">I keep my personal dev folder in Dropbox so I don&#x27;t have to worry about lost work since it always syncs to the cloud.  My work dev folder uses syncthing to a remote server for the same purpose.<p>I make my own branch and then do checkins when I get to good stopping points.  Then I do git squash merges to staging or the group branch when I have nice small updates.  Keeps the public history clean and also lets me revert back to the previous stopping point, while not being worried about lost work.</span>

      <center><a id='up_30712767' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712767&amp;how=up&amp;auth=a32bdfcf4fed688219808aa9751a2ec1e34190db&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712767'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">

                  <span class="commtext c00">I don&#x27;t use commits as &quot;atoms&quot; of the work. Instead, I work at a PR level. I make PRs small and often, and try to make each one a small, self-contained piece of work, usually not more than a day or two.<p>Practically this means that I commit and push whenever I feel like it, but then always squash the PR. At that point I create a clean and informative commit message.<p>However, I leave a lot of context and information in GitHub rather than putting it in large commit messages. I rely heavily on GitHub&#x27;s automatic linking of issues and pull requests for this.</span>

IDEs&#x2F;editors like VS Code make it really easy to stage an individual part of a file for the commit. The CLI way (git add -p) has always been pretty confusing to me.</span>

            <tr class='athing comtr' id='30712567'><td><table border='0'>  <tr>    <td class='ind'indent='0'><img src="s.gif" height="1" width="0"></td><td valign="top" class="votelinks">
      <center><a id='up_30712567' class='clicky' href='vote?id=30712567&amp;how=up&amp;auth=b56a679b1939fe5ae063f878453bc13ce724a76f&amp;goto=item%3Fid%3D30712175#30712567'><div class='votearrow' title='upvote'></div></a></center>    </td><td class="default"><div style="margin-top:2px; margin-bottom:-10px;"><span class="comhead">
          <a href="user?id=jmkr" class="hnuser">jmkr</a> <span class="age" title="2022-03-17T15:57:14"><a href="item?id=30712567">13 hours ago</a></span> <span id="unv_30712567"></span><span class="navs"> | <a href="#30712919" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">prev</a> | <a href="#30712273" class="clicky" aria-hidden="true">next</a></span> <a class="togg clicky" id="30712567" n="1" href="javascript:void(0)">[–]</a><span class="onstory"></span>                  </span></div><br><div class="comment">
                  <span class="commtext c00">1. Create feature branch<p>2. Make changes<p>3. Commit changes at whatever point and write a good commit message about the feature.<p>4. Commit more.<p>5. Create feature&#x2F;branch_rebase<p>6. Squash all the commits.<p>7. Push to fork<p>8. Make pull request<p>9. Make fixes and push those commits per fix.<p>10. Squash when merging back to that first commit.<p>I like having a separate rebase branch because then I have all my work in one branch and the history of what I did.  Then I squash it and get rid of the that history, then maybe rebase main back into it if there we changes since I started the feature.</span>

                  <span class="commtext c00">Create branch, I commit stuff always when my stuff isn&#x27;t actively broken and I can somehow describe what the change did.<p>I push often just to be sure. (This is the modern equivalent of constantly hitting Ctrl-S to save).<p>Squash and clean up before merging to main branch after PR is reviewed and accepted. This makes rolling back the change a lot easier and keeps the main branch log clean from &quot;let&#x27;s see if this crap works&quot; -style messages.</span>

                  <span class="commtext c00">I commit often, push to branches until I&#x27;m satisfied, and for smaller branches often do squash-merges so all of the mess doesn&#x27;t show up in the history.<p>Also, I too used &quot;foo&quot; and &quot;bar&quot; as variable names, but stopped doing that.
